The past five years have seen rapid growth in the contribution of Asian nations to the global fluid and powdered milk manufacturing industry, which in 2008 accounted for more than 30% of production, while China alone accounts for more than 10% of both production and consumption in the global fruit and vegetable processing and preserving industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2013 <a href="/wiki/Overseas_Development_Institute" title="Overseas Development Institute">Overseas Development Institute</a> researchers showed that <a href="/wiki/Rice" title="Rice">rice</a> has more than doubled in price since 2000, rising by 120% in real terms. This was as a result of shifts in trade policy and restocking by major producers. More fundamental drivers of increased prices are the higher costs of fertiliser, diesel and labour. Parts of Asia see rural wages rise with potential large benefits for the 1.3 billion (2008 estimate) of Asia's poor in reducing the poverty they face. However, this negatively impacts more vulnerable groups who don't share in the economic boom, especially in Asian and African coastal cities. The researchers said the threat means social-protection policies are needed to guard against price shocks. The research proposed that in the longer run, the rises present opportunities to export for Western African farmers with high potential for rice production to replace imports with domestic production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most recently, global food prices have been more stable and relatively low, after a sizable increase in late 2017, they are back under 75% of the nominal value seen during the all-time high in the <a href="/wiki/2010%E2%80%9312_world_food_price_crisis" title="2010–12 world food price crisis">2011 food crisis</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="As_investment">As investment</span></h3>
<p>Institutions such as <a href="/wiki/Hedge_fund" title="Hedge fund">hedge funds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pension_fund" title="Pension fund">pension funds</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Investment_bank" title="Investment bank">investment banks</a> like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Barclays_Capital" title="Barclays Capital">Barclays Capital</a>, <a href="/wiki/Goldman_Sachs" title="Goldman Sachs">Goldman Sachs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Morgan_Stanley" title="Morgan Stanley">Morgan Stanley</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ind_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ind-67">[67]</a></sup> have been instrumental in pushing up prices in the last five years, with investment in food <a href="/wiki/Commodity" title="Commodity">commodities</a> rising from $65bn to $126bn (£41bn to £79bn) between 2007 and 2012, contributing to 30-year highs. This has caused price fluctuations which are not strongly related to the actual supply of food, according to the United Nations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ind_67-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ind-67">[67]</a></sup> Financial institutions now make up 61% of all investment in <a href="/wiki/Wheat" title="Wheat">wheat</a> futures. According to <a href="/wiki/Olivier_De_Schutter" title="Olivier De Schutter">Olivier De Schutter</a>, the UN special rapporteur on food, there was a rush by institutions to enter the food market following <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_W_Bush" title="George W Bush">George W Bush</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commodities_Futures_Modernization_Act" title="Commodities Futures Modernization Act">Commodities Futures Modernization Act</a> of 2000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ind_67-3"><a href="#cite_note-Ind-67">[67]</a></sup> De Schutter told the Independent in March 2012: "What we are seeing now is that these financial markets have developed massively with the arrival of these new financial investors, who are purely interested in the short-term monetary gain and are not really interested in the physical thing – they never actually buy the ton of wheat or <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">maize</a>; they only buy a promise to buy or to sell. The result of this financialisation of the commodities market is that the prices of the products respond increasingly to a purely speculative logic. This explains why in very short periods of time we see prices spiking or bubbles exploding, because prices are less and less determined by the real match between supply and demand."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ind_67-4"><a href="#cite_note-Ind-67">[67]</a></sup> In 2011, 450 economists from around the world called on the <a href="/wiki/G20" title="G20">G20</a> to regulate the commodities market more.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ind_67-5"><a href="#cite_note-Ind-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some experts have said that speculation has merely aggravated other factors, such as <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>, competition with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bio-fuels" title="Bio-fuels">bio-fuels</a> and overall rising demand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ind_67-6"><a href="#cite_note-Ind-67">[67]</a></sup> However, some such as <a href="/wiki/Jayati_Ghosh" title="Jayati Ghosh">Jayati Ghosh</a>, professor of economics at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jawaharlal_Nehru_University,_Delhi" title="Jawaharlal Nehru University, Delhi">Jawaharlal Nehru University</a> in <a href="/wiki/New_Delhi" title="New Delhi">New Delhi</a>, have pointed out that prices have increased irrespective of supply and demand issues: Ghosh points to world wheat prices, which doubled in the period from June to December 2010, despite there being no fall in global supply.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ind_67-7"><a href="#cite_note-Ind-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Famine_and_hunger">Famine and hunger</span></h2>
<p>Food deprivation leads to malnutrition and ultimately <a href="/wiki/Starvation" title="Starvation">starvation</a>. This is often connected with <a href="/wiki/Famine" title="Famine">famine</a>, which involves the absence of food in entire communities. This can have a devastating and widespread effect on human health and mortality. <a href="/wiki/Rationing" title="Rationing">Rationing</a> is sometimes used to distribute food in times of shortage, most notably during times of war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Messer_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Messer-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>Starvation is a significant international problem. Approximately 815 million people are undernourished, and over 16,000 children die per day from hunger-related causes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Food deprivation is regarded as a deficit need in <a href="/wiki/Maslow%27s_hierarchy_of_needs" title="Maslow's hierarchy of needs">Maslow's hierarchy of needs</a> and is measured using <a href="/wiki/Famine_scales" title="Famine scales">famine scales</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Food_aid">Food aid</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Food_aid" title="Food aid">Food aid</a></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Food_aid" title="Food aid">Food aid</a> can benefit people suffering from a shortage of food. It can be used to improve peoples' lives in the short term, so that a society can increase its standard of living to the point that food aid is no longer required.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> Conversely, badly managed food aid can create problems by disrupting local markets, depressing crop prices, and discouraging food production. Sometimes a cycle of food aid dependence can develop.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> Its provision, or threatened withdrawal, is sometimes used as a political tool to influence the policies of the destination country, a strategy known as <a href="/wiki/Food_politics" title="Food politics">food politics</a>. Sometimes, food aid provisions will require certain types of food be purchased from certain sellers, and food aid can be misused to enhance the markets of donor countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> International efforts to distribute food to the neediest countries are often coordinated by the <a href="/wiki/World_Food_Programme" title="World Food Programme">World Food Programme</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Safety">Safety</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Food_safety" title="Food safety">Food safety</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SalmonellaNIAID.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1761" data-file-width="2100" height="184" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/SalmonellaNIAID.jpg/220px-SalmonellaNIAID.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/SalmonellaNIAID.jpg/330px-SalmonellaNIAID.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/SalmonellaNIAID.jpg/440px-SalmonellaNIAID.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SalmonellaNIAID.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Salmonella" title="Salmonella">Salmonella</a> bacteria is a common cause of foodborne illness, particularly in undercooked <a href="/wiki/Chicken" title="Chicken">chicken</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chicken_egg" title="Chicken egg">chicken eggs</a>.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Foodborne_illness" title="Foodborne illness">Foodborne illness</a>, commonly called "food poisoning", is caused by <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Toxin" title="Toxin">toxins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">viruses</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parasite" title="Parasite">parasites</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Prion" title="Prion">prions</a>. Roughly 7 million people die of food poisoning each year, with about 10 times as many suffering from a non-fatal version.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MedlinePlus_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-MedlinePlus-79">[79]</a></sup> The two most common factors leading to cases of bacterial foodborne illness are cross-contamination of ready-to-eat food from other uncooked foods and improper temperature control. Less commonly, acute adverse reactions can also occur if chemical contamination of food occurs, for example from improper storage, or use of non-food grade soaps and disinfectants. Food can also be adulterated by a very wide range of articles (known as "foreign bodies") during farming, manufacture, cooking, packaging, distribution, or sale. These foreign bodies can include pests or their droppings, hairs, cigarette butts, wood chips, and all manner of other contaminants. It is possible for certain types of food to become contaminated if stored or presented in an unsafe container, such as a ceramic pot with lead-based glaze.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MedlinePlus_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-MedlinePlus-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Food_poisoning" title="Food poisoning">Food poisoning</a> has been recognized as a disease since as early as Hippocrates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> The sale of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rancidity" title="Rancidity">rancid</a>, contaminated, or adulterated food was commonplace until the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Hygiene" title="Hygiene">hygiene</a>, refrigeration, and vermin controls in the 19th century. Discovery of techniques for killing bacteria using heat, and other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microbiological" title="Microbiological">microbiological</a> studies by scientists such as <a href="/wiki/Louis_Pasteur" title="Louis Pasteur">Louis Pasteur</a>, contributed to the modern sanitation standards that are ubiquitous in developed nations today. This was further underpinned by the work of <a href="/wiki/Justus_von_Liebig" title="Justus von Liebig">Justus von Liebig</a>, which led to the development of modern <a href="/wiki/Food_storage" title="Food storage">food storage</a> and <a href="/wiki/Food_preservation" title="Food preservation">food preservation</a> methods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> In more recent years, a greater understanding of the causes of food-borne illnesses has led to the development of more systematic approaches such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hazard_Analysis_and_Critical_Control_Points" title="Hazard Analysis and Critical Control Points">Hazard Analysis and Critical Control Points</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HACCP" title="HACCP">HACCP</a>), which can identify and eliminate many risks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>Recommended measures for ensuring food safety include maintaining a clean preparation area with foods of different types kept separate, ensuring an adequate cooking temperature, and refrigerating foods promptly after cooking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>Foods that spoil easily, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meats" title="Meats">meats</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dairy" title="Dairy">dairy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Seafood" title="Seafood">seafood</a>, must be prepared a certain way to avoid contaminating the people for whom they are prepared. As such, the rule of thumb is that cold foods (such as dairy products) should be kept cold and hot foods (such as soup) should be kept hot until storage. Cold meats, such as chicken, that are to be cooked should not be placed at room temperature for thawing, at the risk of dangerous bacterial growth, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Salmonella" title="Salmonella">Salmonella</a></i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Escherichia_coli" title="Escherichia coli">E. coli</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Allergies">Allergies</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Food_allergy" title="Food allergy">Food allergy</a></div>
<p>Some people have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Allergies" title="Allergies">allergies</a> or sensitivities to foods which are not problematic to most people. This occurs when a person's <a href="/wiki/Immune_system" title="Immune system">immune system</a> mistakes a certain food protein for a harmful foreign agent and attacks it. About 2% of adults and 8% of children have a food allergy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allergy_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-allergy-85">[85]</a></sup> The amount of the food substance required to provoke a reaction in a particularly susceptible individual can be quite small. In some instances, traces of food in the air, too minute to be perceived through smell, have been known to provoke lethal reactions in extremely sensitive individuals. Common food allergens are <a href="/wiki/Gluten" title="Gluten">gluten</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">corn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shellfish" title="Shellfish">shellfish</a> (mollusks), <a href="/wiki/Peanut" title="Peanut">peanuts</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soy" title="Soy">soy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allergy_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-allergy-85">[85]</a></sup> Allergens frequently produce symptoms such as <a href="/wiki/Diarrhea" title="Diarrhea">diarrhea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rash" title="Rash">rashes</a>, bloating, <a href="/wiki/Vomiting" title="Vomiting">vomiting</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Regurgitation_(digestion)" title="Regurgitation (digestion)">regurgitation</a>. The digestive complaints usually develop within half an hour of ingesting the <a href="/wiki/Allergen" title="Allergen">allergen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allergy_85-2"><a href="#cite_note-allergy-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>Rarely, food allergies can lead to a <a href="/wiki/Medical_emergency" title="Medical emergency">medical emergency</a>, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anaphylactic_shock" title="Anaphylactic shock">anaphylactic shock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hypotension" title="Hypotension">hypotension</a> (low blood pressure), and loss of consciousness. An allergen associated with this type of reaction is peanut, although <a href="/wiki/Latex" title="Latex">latex</a> products can induce similar reactions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allergy_85-3"><a href="#cite_note-allergy-85">[85]</a></sup> Initial treatment is with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epinephrine" title="Epinephrine">epinephrine</a> (adrenaline), often carried by known patients in the form of an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epi-pen" title="Epi-pen">Epi-pen</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Twinject" title="Twinject">Twinject</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_health_issues">Other health issues</span></h3>
<p>Human diet was estimated to cause perhaps around 35% of <a href="/wiki/Diet_and_cancer" title="Diet and cancer">cancers</a> in a human epidemiological analysis by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Doll" title="Richard Doll">Richard Doll</a> and <a href="/wiki/Richard_Peto" title="Richard Peto">Richard Peto</a> in 1981.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup>  These cancer may be caused by <a href="/wiki/Carcinogen" title="Carcinogen">carcinogens</a> that are present in food naturally or as contaminants. Food contaminated with fungal growth may contain <a href="/wiki/Mycotoxin" title="Mycotoxin">mycotoxins</a> such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aflatoxins" title="Aflatoxins">aflatoxins</a> which may be found in contaminated corn and peanuts.  Other carcinogens identified in food include <a href="/wiki/Heterocyclic_amine" title="Heterocyclic amine">heterocyclic amines</a> generated in meat when cooked at high temperature, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polyaromatic_hydrocarbon" title="Polyaromatic hydrocarbon">polyaromatic hydrocarbons</a> in charred meat and smoked fish, and <a href="/wiki/Nitrosamine" title="Nitrosamine">nitrosamines</a> generated from nitrites used as food preservatives in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cured_meat" title="Cured meat">cured meat</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Bacon" title="Bacon">bacon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-carcinogens_and_anticarcinogens_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-carcinogens_and_anticarcinogens-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Anticarcinogen" title="Anticarcinogen">Anticarcinogens</a> that may help prevent cancer can also be found in many food especially fruit and vegetables. <a href="/wiki/Antioxidant" title="Antioxidant">Antioxidants</a> are important groups of compounds that may help remove potentially harmful chemicals. It is however often difficult to identify the specific components in diet that serve to increase or decrease cancer risk since many food, such as beef steak and broccoli, contain low concentrations of both carcinogens and anticarcinogens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-carcinogens_and_anticarcinogens_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-carcinogens_and_anticarcinogens-89">[89]</a></sup>
There are many international certifications in cooking field, such as <a href="/wiki/Monde_Selection" title="Monde Selection">Monde Selection</a>、A.A.Certification、iTQi. They use the high quality evaluation methods to make the food become more safe.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Diet">Diet</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FAO_kcal_his.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="433" data-file-width="625" height="208" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/FAO_kcal_his.png/300px-FAO_kcal_his.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/FAO_kcal_his.png/450px-FAO_kcal_his.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/FAO_kcal_his.png/600px-FAO_kcal_his.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:FAO_kcal_his.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Changes of food supply (by energy)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup><br/>Other area (Yr 2010)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> * Africa, sub-Sahara - 2170 kcal/capita/day * N.E. and N. Africa - 3120 kcal/capita/day * South Asia - 2450 kcal/capita/day * East Asia - 3040 kcal/capita/day * Latin America / Caribbean - 2950 kcal/capita/day * Developed countries - 3470 kcal/capita/day</div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Diet_(nutrition)" title="Diet (nutrition)">Diet (nutrition)</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_and_religious_diets">Cultural and religious diets</span></h3>
<p>Many cultures hold some food preferences and some food <a href="/wiki/Taboo" title="Taboo">taboos</a>. Dietary choices can also define cultures and play a role in religion. For example, only <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kosher" title="Kosher">kosher foods</a> are permitted by <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Halal" title="Halal">halal</a> foods by <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>, and in <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a> <a href="/wiki/Beef" title="Beef">beef</a> is restricted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> In addition, the dietary choices of different countries or regions have different characteristics. This is highly related to a culture's <a href="/wiki/Cuisine" title="Cuisine">cuisine</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diet_deficiencies">Diet deficiencies</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Avitaminosis" title="Avitaminosis">Avitaminosis</a></div>
<p>Dietary habits play a significant role in the health and mortality of all humans. Imbalances between the consumed fuels and expended energy results in either starvation or excessive reserves of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adipose" title="Adipose">adipose</a> tissue, known as body fat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> Poor intake of various vitamins and minerals can lead to diseases that can have far-reaching effects on health. For instance, 30% of the world's population either has, or is at risk for developing, <a href="/wiki/Iodine_deficiency" title="Iodine deficiency">iodine deficiency</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> It is estimated that at least 3 million children are blind due to <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_A" title="Vitamin A">vitamin A</a> deficiency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_C" title="Vitamin C">Vitamin C</a> deficiency results in <a href="/wiki/Scurvy" title="Scurvy">scurvy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">Calcium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_D" title="Vitamin D">Vitamin D</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a> are inter-related; the consumption of each may affect the absorption of the others. <a href="/wiki/Kwashiorkor" title="Kwashiorkor">Kwashiorkor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marasmus" title="Marasmus">marasmus</a> are childhood disorders caused by lack of dietary protein.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Moral.2C_ethical.2C_and_health-conscious_diets"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Moral,_ethical,_and_health-conscious_diets">Moral, ethical, and health-conscious diets</span></h3>
<p>Many individuals limit what foods they eat for reasons of morality, or other habit. For instance, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vegetarian" title="Vegetarian">vegetarians</a> choose to forgo food from animal sources to varying degrees. Others choose a <a href="/wiki/Healthy_diet" title="Healthy diet">healthier diet</a>, avoiding sugars or animal fats and increasing consumption of dietary fiber and <a href="/wiki/Antioxidant" title="Antioxidant">antioxidants</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Obesity" title="Obesity">Obesity</a>, a serious problem in the western world, leads to higher chances of developing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heart_disease" title="Heart disease">heart disease</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diabetes" title="Diabetes">diabetes</a>, cancer and many other diseases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> More recently, dietary habits have been influenced by the concerns that some people have about possible impacts on health or the environment from <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_food" title="Genetically modified food">genetically modified food</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> Further concerns about the impact of industrial farming (<a href="/wiki/Cereal" title="Cereal">grains</a>) on <a href="/wiki/Animal_welfare" title="Animal welfare">animal welfare</a>, human health, and the <a href="/wiki/Ecology" title="Ecology">environment</a> are also having an effect on contemporary human dietary habits. This has led to the emergence of a movement with a preference for <a href="/wiki/Organic_food" title="Organic food">organic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Local_food" title="Local food">local food</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nutrition_and_dietary_problems">Nutrition and dietary problems</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:USDA_MyPlate_green.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="199" data-file-width="217" height="202" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/USDA_MyPlate_green.svg/220px-USDA_MyPlate_green.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/USDA_MyPlate_green.svg/330px-USDA_MyPlate_green.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/USDA_MyPlate_green.svg/440px-USDA_MyPlate_green.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:USDA_MyPlate_green.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/MyPlate" title="MyPlate">MyPlate</a> replaced <a href="/wiki/MyPyramid" title="MyPyramid">MyPyramid</a> as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USDA" title="USDA">USDA</a> nutrition guide.</div></div></div>
<p>Between the extremes of optimal health and death from <a href="/wiki/Starvation" title="Starvation">starvation</a> or <a href="/wiki/Malnutrition" title="Malnutrition">malnutrition</a>, there is an array of disease states that can be caused or alleviated by changes in diet. Deficiencies, excesses, and imbalances in diet can produce negative impacts on health, which may lead to various health problems such as <a href="/wiki/Scurvy" title="Scurvy">scurvy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Obesity" title="Obesity">obesity</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Osteoporosis" title="Osteoporosis">osteoporosis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diabetes_mellitus_type_2" title="Diabetes mellitus type 2">diabetes</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cardiovascular_diseases" title="Cardiovascular diseases">cardiovascular diseases</a> as well as psychological and behavioral problems. The science of nutrition attempts to understand how and why specific dietary aspects influence health.
</p><p>Nutrients in food are grouped into several categories. Macronutrients are fat, protein, and carbohydrates. Micronutrients are the <a href="/wiki/Mineral_(nutrient)" title="Mineral (nutrient)">minerals</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vitamins" title="Vitamins">vitamins</a>. Additionally, food contains water and <a href="/wiki/Dietary_fiber" title="Dietary fiber">dietary fiber</a>.
</p><p>As previously discussed, the body is designed by <a href="/wiki/Natural_selection" title="Natural selection">natural selection</a> to enjoy sweet and fattening foods for evolutionary diets, ideal for <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherer" title="Hunter-gatherer">hunters and gatherers</a>. Thus, sweet and fattening foods in nature are typically rare and are very pleasurable to eat. In modern times, with <a href="/wiki/Biotechnology" title="Biotechnology">advanced technology</a>, enjoyable foods are easily available to consumers. Unfortunately, this promotes <a href="/wiki/Obesity" title="Obesity">obesity</a> in adults and children alike.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legal_definition">Legal definition</span></h2>
<p>Some countries list a legal definition of food, often referring them with the word <i>foodstuff</i>. These countries list food as any item that is to be processed, partially processed, or unprocessed for consumption. The listing of items included as food include any substance intended to be, or reasonably expected to be, ingested by <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">humans</a>. In addition to these foodstuffs, drink, <a href="/wiki/Chewing_gum" title="Chewing gum">chewing gum</a>, water, or other items processed into said food items are part of the legal definition of food. Items not included in the legal definition of food include <a href="/wiki/Pet_food" title="Pet food">animal feed</a>, live animals (unless being prepared for sale in a market), plants prior to harvesting, medicinal products, <a href="/wiki/Cosmetics" title="Cosmetics">cosmetics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tobacco" title="Tobacco">tobacco</a> and tobacco products, <a href="/wiki/Narcotic" title="Narcotic">narcotic</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Psychotropic_substance" title="Psychotropic substance">psychotropic substances</a>, and residues and contaminants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-20"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-20">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The <a href="/wiki/Sweetness" title="Sweetness">sweetness</a> multiplier "300 times" comes from <a class="external text" href="http://www.science.edu.sg/ssc/detailed.jsp?artid=67&amp;type=6&amp;root=3&amp;parent=3&amp;cat=30" rel="nofollow">subjective evaluations by a panel of test subjects</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20090123124043/http://www.science.edu.sg/ssc/detailed.jsp?artid=67&amp;type=6&amp;root=3&amp;parent=3&amp;cat=30" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> January 23, 2009, at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>. tasting various dilutions compared to a standard dilution of <a href="/wiki/Sucrose" title="Sucrose">sucrose</a>. Sources referenced in this article say steviosides have up to 250 times the sweetness of sucrose, but others, including stevioside brands such as SweetLeaf, claim 300 times. 1/3 to 1/2 teaspoon (1.6–2.5 ml) of stevioside powder is claimed to have equivalent sweetening power to 1 cup (237 ml) of sugar.</span>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Further_reading">Further reading</span></h2>
<ul><li>Collingham, E. M. (2011).  <i>The Taste of War: World War Two and the Battle for Food</i></li>
<li>Katz, Solomon (2003). <i>The Encyclopedia of Food and Culture</i>, Scribner</li>
<li>Nestle, Marion (2007). <i>Food Politics: How the Food Industry Influences Nutrition and Health</i>, University Presses of California, revised and expanded edition, <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-520-25403-1" title="Special:BookSources/0-520-25403-1">0-520-25403-1</a></li>
<li>Mobbs, Michael (2012). <i>Sustainable Food</i> Sydney: NewSouth Publishing,  <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9781920705541" title="Special:BookSources/9781920705541">9781920705541</a></li>
<li><a class="external text" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=VQsC7e2qAcY" rel="nofollow">The Future of Food</a> (2015).  A panel discussion at the 2015  <i><a href="/wiki/Digital_Life_Design" title="Digital Life Design">Digital Life Design</a> (DLD)</i> Annual Conference. "How can we grow and enjoy food, closer to home, further into the future? <i><a href="/wiki/MIT_Media_Lab" title="MIT Media Lab">MIT Media Lab</a>’s</i> Kevin Slavin hosts a conversation with food artist, educator, and entrepreneur Emilie Baltz, professor Caleb Harper from <i>MIT Media Lab's</i> CityFarm project, the Barbarian Group's Benjamin Palmer, and Andras Forgacs, the co-founder and CEO of <i>Modern Meadow</i>, who is growing 'victimless' meat in a lab. The discussion addresses issues of sustainable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urban_farming" title="Urban farming">urban farming</a>, ecosystems, technology, food supply chains and their broad environmental and humanitarian implications, and how these changes in food production may change what people may find delicious ... and the other way around."  Posted on the official YouTube Channel of <i><a href="/wiki/Digital_Life_Design" title="Digital Life Design">DLD</a></i></li></ul>
